Automated and optimized design of steel structures in fire
iii
==PAGE:5==
Abstract
Optimization is a major driving factor in the construction industry that focuses on
cost minimization. Steel structures are favored and are one of the favorites of
architects. However, steel suffers from a critical safety factor, fire resistance.
Steel structures are usually optimized to carry the mechanical loads and comply
with maximum allowed displacements using the minimum amount of steel. This
method keeps the steel cost to a minimum but does not consider the accidental
limit state regarding fire verification. Using traditional design methods, the total
price of steel and fire insulation is unconsidered, resulting in a potential loss of
optimization cost. This thesis investigates possible options for optimizing steel
structures while also considering fire verification.
Previous studies have shown beneficial effects for design safety and savings of
material costs when optimization includes fire verification. However, fire verifica­
tion is usually calculated last in the design phase, leaving no room for optimizing.
Previous studies also highlight that the fire verification must be included at the
start of the design stage to be relevant.
The thesis aims to advance the optimization presented in (Martin Thaarup, 2019)
and solve the optimization problem regarding a steel member and its correspond­
ing fire protection. Based on design methods presented in Eurocode 3, boundary
conditions for the optimization problem are selected. Due to a misleading bound­
ary condition for columns, the focus of the thesis is limited to beams.
To solve the optimization problem, an analytical framework is presented. The
framework investigates and later solves how to determine the critical steel tem­
perature of an element given the reduction factor ξ and determine the needed
thickness of insulation din analytically.
To efficiently use optimization, a script is developed in Python and implemented
in the existing structural fire design software SteFi. A Graphical User Interface is
built in for easy use of the optimization module. For realistic optimization, a library
of standard profiles is included.
A prediction model determining the modified section factor is developed for limited
parametric fire properties. The prediction model is verified by comparison to the
thickness of insulation calculated using the spreadsheet method with differences
of less than 6%.
To test the optimization, a case study is conducted based on an experimental open
car park. Three designs are compared to analyze potential savings and fire safety.
The case study also discussed when optimization is beneficial.
The optimized car park designed using a parametric fire increases the cost by
26% compared to the car park designed with a local fire. The potential savings for
the case study is 5% for beams compared to a traditional design with insulation.
Further analysis concludes potential savings of 11% compared to a traditional
design for steel structures if the cost of the fire protection is equal to or larger than
the cost of steel.
Automated and optimized design of steel structures in fire

==PAGE:13==
1
Introduction
Optimization has always been a key factor for civil engineers. The ability to limit
material consumption have had great positive outcomes, from times when trans­
port options to a building site were limited to the present, where decreasing cost
and environmental impact is key. The optimization has mostly been achieved by
reducing structure weight or volume (Hao et al., 2015), but this structural optimiza­
tion does not always benefit the structure.
1.1
Motivation
The importance of structural optimization has increased rapidly after the year 2000,
where 62% of research articles have been centered around cost minimization (Mei
and Q. Wang, 2021). From 2006 to 2020, the amount of structural optimization
research focusing on structural performance improvement has increased from 5%
to 22%, hence showing the importance of safety and serviceability of a structural
system (Mei and Q. Wang, 2021).
The increased awareness regarding structural performance improvement is es­
pecially noticeable for steel. Previously and still today structural optimization for
steel is done by reducing the amount of steel, as it is the most important factor
for the cost­saving (Hao et al., 2015). One of the reasons that steel once again
has become popular is due to the total cost of steel products, and its architectural
possibilities. Steel’s popularity can easily be recognized in figure 1.1.
Figure 1.1: Market share of structural frames in multi­storey buildings in the UK
(1980 to 2020) (BCSA, Steel for Life, and SCI, 2022b).
Automated and optimized design of steel structures in fire
1
==PAGE:14==
1.2. OBJECTIVE
Chapter 1.
From 1980 to 2020, the UK market share of steel frames for multi­story buildings
increased from 33% to 65%, while concrete has fallen from 53% to 24%.
A study from 2012 by (BCSA et. al.) has shown that the cost of steel­framed op­
tions for typical office building types is potentially up to 9% lower than for concrete.
The study also emphasized the importance of the total construction cost and not
only the cost of the structural frame. It was found that the total building cost for the
steel options was on average around 5% lower than the concrete options (BCSA,
Tata steel, and Gardiner & Theobald, 2012).
Steel is also known to be one of the architect’s favorite material as it makes the
designs of modern architecture possible. This is due to the fact that steel has
the best strength­to­weight ratio, has great recycling abilities, as well as its ver­
satility gives architects freedom to be visionary. Steel as a construction material
has many great advantages compared to other materials. However, it is weak in
terms of a critical safety factor, fire resistance. Materials such as concrete and
wood have better fire resistance than steel and are often opted for due to total
cost, safety, and structural performance in fire. Steels fire performance has been
studied more than any other building material and can be increased by applying
fire protection (BCSA, Steel for Life, and SCI, 2022b). The reason why steel is
not chosen instead of concrete and wood is the cost of fire protection. The most
used fire protection product according to (Steel Construction, 2022) is intumescent
paint. The paint represents 80% of the market in 2017. The price of intumescent
paint is roughly 50 times more expensive than steel.
Steel structures are yet to be optimized based on a combination of steel and fire
protection design. Steel structures are usually optimized to carry the mechani­
cal loads and comply with maximum allowed displacements using the minimum
amount of steel. This method keeps the steel cost to a minimum but does not
consider the accidental limit state regarding fire verification. This often results in
high costs regarding fire insulation of the structure, neutralizing the original steel
optimization.
Using traditional design methods, the total price of steel and fire insulation is never
considered, meaning that the optimization performed in terms of cost on the steel
structure is usually lost.
1.2
Objective
The thesis aims to analyze and solve a cost optimization problem regarding a
steel element and its corresponding fire protection. An analytical approach to
an optimization problem shall be conducted using theory for heating of steel and
structural fire design of beams and columns. Secondly, an optimization process
based on the minimization of the total cost of steel and insulation shall be devel­
oped and implemented in the existing software SteFi. For easy use, a Graphical
User Interface must be developed. A library of common steel profiles must be
created for easy comparison. The final aim is to show that using cost optimization
function, it is possible to minimize the total cost of a steel structure and highlight
potential savings. The pricing of steel and fire protection will be a key element in
terms of optimization.
Automated and optimized design of steel structures in fire
2
==PAGE:15==
1.3. METHOD
Chapter 1.
1.3
Method
The theory of designing steel structures and fire protection will be covered along
with current design practice, cost of materials, optimization, and methods to cal­
culate the resistance of steel beams and columns. The governing parameters for
optimization will be highlighted, and later used in the optimization process. The
methods described in this thesis is aimed to be universally understood and used.
The theories used are based on Eurocode, the degradation model by (Kristian
Dahl Hertz, 2019) and Fire Engineering Design of Steel Structures by (Petters­
son, O., 1976). The Danish national annex for Eurocodes will be used.
1.3.1
Optimization algorithm
The method for this thesis is to define a simple analytical approach that can be
used to verify beams and columns affected by fire and use this simple analytic
approach to optimize the structure. It has previously been shown that a total ana­
lytical approach is very complex. To simplify matters, the analytical approach will
be divided into finding the critical temperature of the steel element and determining
the thickness of insulation.
Finding the steel temperature will be based on the reduction factors presented
by Eurocode, (Kristian Dahl Hertz, 2019) and the critical steel temperature for­
mula dependent on the degree of utilization presented in (DS/EN 1993­1­2 +AC
2007). Determining the thickness of insulation has previously been done for the
standard fire curve using nomograms. This method will be adopted to determine
the thickness of insulation for a parametric fire. A possible solution will be verified
using a simple benchmark against the spreadsheet method. In case the analyti­
cal solution is too complex, a numerical approach is introduced to overcome this
challenge. It will mainly be used to obtain the steel temperature Ts and insulation
thickness din.
1.3.2
Development and Coding in Python
The optimization algorithm will be integrated into the existing software SteFi which
is written in Python, by previous DTU students (Mikkel Dohm Andersen and Thomas
Dyhr, 2018). The existing software SteFi is an automatic verification of steel ele­
ments in fire, that is still under development.
Python is a free and open­source programming language, which can be run on
mainstream operating systems. Improvements, bug fixes, and updates will also
be coded in the Python language, as it is easy to use, powerful, and compatible
with previously written code.
SteFi will be built of five scripts; the four originals BackEnd, Report, stefi, setup are
modified to include new features and the new Optimization script. The previous
version 2.0.0 of SteFi includes a BIM integration module, which is kept as it is.
Vision 2.1.0 shall have Excel import and export integrated, for quicker use of the
software.

==PAGE:16==
1.3. METHOD
Chapter 1.
Figure 1.2: Coding language and extensions.
The new optimization module must have a Graphical User Interface (GUI) to be
able to take and give user commands. A GUI can be described as an on­screen
interface that a user can interacte with to execute different commands. To be
able to create an visual interface, the GUI package Tkinter will be used, which is
an extension to Python. The reason for using the GUI package Tkinter is that it
excels at visual representation.
While running the GUI for the application, an updated calculation module and new
optimization module will be executed in the ”backend” (Background) via inputs
and commands from the GUI.
To create a program application, a Python extension called py2exe will be used.
Py2exe converts Python scripts into executable Windows programs.
1.3.3
Case studies
To determine the efficiency and verify that the optimization algorithm is working
correctly and successfully, two case studies will be conducted. The first case
study is used to verify that the optimization calculation returns the same results
as numerical calculations. The first case is a beam based on Problem 1 from the
E2020 exam in 11023 ­ Structural fire safety design with modification of the steel
profile and fire properties. This case study is chosen because of its simplicity and
relevance.
The second case study is a car park based on (Joyeux, 2002). This case study
allows comparison to the original structure and discussion of the fire safety level.
The fire protection used for the car park is intumescent paint. The case study
includes three designs, non­protected, protected, and optimized. This case study
is chosen to show the optimization of a full structure and discover limitations and
potential savings.
Both case studies will be conducted with the Danish fire curve presented in the
danish national annex for (DS/EN 1993­1­2 +AC 2007, EC3­1­2).
Automated and optimized design of steel structures in fire
4
==PAGE:17==
2
Structural fire design
2.1
Current design practice
Structural steel design has been practiced since the late 1800s (LLC, 2018), with
the construction of the Home Insurance Building in Chicago and the Eiffel Tower
in Paris as some examples. Since then, the design of steel structures has been
regulated worldwide, leading to standards such as Eurocode 3: Design of Steel
Structures and national annexes. The standards have been updated continuously
as new knowledge is approved. Simply designing steel structures often depends
on the architecture. In Denmark, (Teknisk Ståbi, udgave 24. 2016, Teknisk Ståbi)
and (Bjarne Chr. Jensen, 2015, Stålkonstruktioner efter DS/EN 1993) are widely
regarded as the ”preferred” books for designing and optimising steel.
Neither of these books has a chapter on the design of fire protection for steel
members. (Teknisk Ståbi, udgave 24. 2016, Teknisk Ståbi) does inform about crit­
ical steel temperature of tensioned/buckling elements and concentrically loaded
columns in relation to the utilization factor in fire using equation (4.22) in (DS/EN
1993­1­2 +AC 2007). If the deformation criteria or stability phenomena shall be
taken into account, then the critical steel temperature is more complicated and
cannot be determined by equation (DS/EN 1993­1­2 +AC 2007).
Normally a structural engineer will design a steel structure using these books and
leave the fire protection to the fire engineer. This process limits the optimization
of the steel profiles.
If a structural engineer were to design a steel structure with fire protection, the
steel would most likely be optimized regarding the ultimate limit state ’ULS’, and
the fire insulation designed to secure the steel capacity in case of fire. This pro­
cess might also lead to a nonoptimal design regarding the use and cost of re­
sources.
2.1.1
Profile types
There are many different profile sections available in the building industry. The
most used profile is an I­profile, where the web resists shear forces, while the
flanges resist most of the bending moment. I­profiles come in different shapes,
HE­A, HE­B, INP, IPE, etc. This profile type is a very efficient beam for carrying
both bending and shear loads as shown by the Euler–Bernoulli beam equation.
I­profiles are also used as columns.
Hollow sections come as squared (SHS), rectangular (RHS), or circular (CHS)
and are used when there is loading in a variety of directions, as these profiles are
strong in both directions. The profiles are also good against torsion. SHS and
RHS sections are especially useful because of its flat surfaces resulting in easy
structural solutions that are more economical for joining elements (Resources,
2019).
Automated and optimized design of steel structures in fire
5
==PAGE:18==
2.2. DESIGN FIRES
Chapter 2.
C­beams (also known as Channel beams) are created by a top and bottom flange,
forming a C shape. C­beams have been known for their use in bridges. However,
C­beams are beginning to be used in various construction applications, including
balconies, because of their capacity and flat surface.
L­shaped beams are most suitable for floor slab systems with building foundations.
They are popular as their L­shape provides great stability to the floor system while
reducing structural depth (Midwest Steel, 2017).
This thesis will only consider I­profiles and hollow sections for optimization, as
these are the most common profiles used in structural steel structures. Custom
I­profiles and hollow sections will also be considered.
2.2
Design fires
For each building space, a fire curve can be designed to indicate temperature
changes over time. This kind of curve is a useful tool for designing fire safety for
buildings. The fire curves are used to express the gas temperature varying with
time.
The case study will be analyzed with both standard and parametric fire. The fol­
lowing sections describe the two design fires.
2.2.1
Standard fire
The standard fire curve (also known as ISO834 curve) is used for fire resistance
testing of components and is acknowledged internationally. This fire curve is a
nominal time­temperature curve.
The gas temperature given by the standard fire curve is found by the following
equation (3.4) in (DS/EN 1991­1­2 2007):
Tg = 20 + 345 · log10 (8 · t + 1)
(2.1)
This fire curve is based on the burning rate of building materials and interior con­
tents. The standard fire curve is assumed to be conservative in terms of tem­
perature development. The standard fire curve can be used for all fire design
scenarios.
2.2.2
Parametric fire
A parametric fire curve is based on a simplified fire model, and depends on multi­
ple properties, hence performance­based. A parametric fire curve is also a time­
temperature curve, characterized by including a heating phase, reaches a post­
flashover phase where the fire­load density is completely burned­out and a cooling
phase. A parametric fire curve is only valid for a compartment with a floor area of
500 m2 or less, without openings in the roof and a maximum compartment height
of 4 m. The opening factor O is limited to values [0.02 −0.20] m−1 and the thermal
inertia b is limited to values [100 −2200] J/m2s½K. Normal values of the fuel load
q is [100 −300] MJ/m2. The parametric fire curve used in Denmark is defined in
(DS/EN 1991­1­2 DK NA:2014 2014).

2.3
Steels thermal response
Steel together with all other construction materials loses its strength at elevated
temperatures. To calculate the change in resistance with temperature, it is neces­
sary to know why and what affects the strength reduction of the material. It is also
important to understand how quickly a structural member reaches a critical tem­
perature at which it will no longer be able to support the loads in fire conditions.
Various studies show that it is both thermal and mechanical properties of steel
that lead to strength reduction at elevated temperatures. The thermal properties
Automated and optimized design of steel structures in fire
7
==PAGE:20==
2.3. STEELS THERMAL RESPONSE
Chapter 2.
of steel are known due to heat transfer analysis. It is found that the specific heat
and the thermal conductivity are the most important thermal properties of steel.
To simplify calculations, it is usually assumed that the thermal properties of steel
remain constant. However specific heat, density, and thermal conductivity do vary
with temperature. The density of steel ρs is however recommended by (Purkiss
and L.­Y. Li, 2014) and (DS/EN 1993­1­2 +AC 2007) to be a constant value of
7850 kg/m3.
Thermal conductivity of steel will not be discussed or used in this thesis.
Steel as a material is considered an elastoplastic material in the ultimate limit state.
The yield stress for carbon steel is defined as the stress of where a straight line
with the inclination of the Youngs modulus from the point of 0.2%strain intersects
the stress­strain curve (Kristian Dahl Hertz, 2019). Determining the yield stress at
elevated temperature is a different story, as there is a lack of a defined yield point
(Simms, 2012) (seen in figure 2.2).
Figure 2.2: Normalised stress strain curves for steel at elevated temperature in
(Simms, 2012, figure 5.4).
This behavior of steel at elevated temperature has given reason to adopt a strain
limit of 2%. This is reasonable based on fire tests showing that steel members
can have a total strain over 3% when they reach the limiting deflection. However,
for class 4 section, the local buckling usually limits the strain, resulting in strength
reduction factors based on a strain limit of 0.5% (Simms, 2012). A strain of 2% is
not possible in columns as they would fail due to large deflections (Kristian Dahl
Hertz, 2019).
Steel at elevated temperatures can be calculated with the effective yield strength
with a strain of 2% or using the design yield strength with a strain of 0.2%. Deter­
mining which yield strength to use depends on the class sections and the structure
Automated and optimized design of steel structures in fire
8
==PAGE:21==
2.3. STEELS THERMAL RESPONSE
Chapter 2.
possibility to absorb the large deformations that 2% strain will cause. The Dan­
ish national annex (DS/EN 1993­1­2 DK NA:2007 2007) to Eurocode 3 part 1­2,
states the following (translate to English):
”The ”effective yielding”, as shown in Table 3.1, requires a strain of at least 2.0%.
At full utilization of this strain, it must be required that the structure can absorb
the large deformations that a 2.0% strain will cause. This includes the integrity
and mounting of fire insulation, compression, and destruction of underlying fire­
separating walls, etc. A simplified consideration of deformations can be made by
using a 0.2% strain for structural steel corresponding to the values in Table E1.”
The 0.2% strain is shown on figure 2.3.
Figure 2.3: Reduction factors for the stress­strain relationship of cold formed and
holt rolled class 4 steel sections at elevated temperatures (DS/EN 1993­1­2 +AC
2007).
The static system with large deformations caused by 2.0% strain shall behave the
same as with small deformations. This statement/definition is however vague and
is hard to prove, as steel rarely reaches 2.0% strain. Due to this, most structural
designers use effective yield strength with 2.0% strain.
2.3.1
Specific heat of steel
Specific heat of a material is the energy from the heat required to raise the tem­
perature of a unit mass by 1K and is measured in J/kg K (Simms, 2012). The
specific heat of steel ca is the thermal property of steel which has the largest devi­
ation from a constant value. (DS/EN 1993­1­2 +AC 2007) determine the specific
heat of carbon steel by the following equations:

ceptable as temperatures above 600◦C are rarely used as the strength is heavily
reduced.
2.3.2
Heating of steel
To determine the strength reduction at elevated temperatures, the temperature of
the steel element shall be known. The temperature is time­dependent as the gas
temperature in section 2.2. The temperature can be determined by finite element
computer programs or by a one­dimensional heat flow analysis, based on iteration,
also called the spreadsheet method.
Automated and optimized design of steel structures in fire
10
==PAGE:23==
2.3. STEELS THERMAL RESPONSE
Chapter 2.
The spreadsheet method is the most common approach for simple structural fire
design. The temperature of a steel element is calculated at each time step, by
considering the energy that the beam is subjected to during the previous time
interval. If the profile is protected or not, the equations to calculate the temperature
is listed in the following sections.
Common for all methods determining the maximum steel temperature is the calcu­
lations. It is assumed that the temperature distribution is uniform throughout the
cross­section.

2.3.3
Steel reduction factor
Heating steel elements leads to a reduction in the yield strength fy and modulus of
elasticity E. The reduction of material properties is treated differently depending
on the chosen material model. In this thesis, reduction according to (DS/EN 1993­
1­2 +AC 2007, EC3­1­2) and (Kristian Dahl Hertz, 2019, Hertz)) will be considered.
The material models are presented below.
Reduction according to Eurocode [EC3-1-2]
Eurocode 3 applies reduction factors for both the stiffness and the yield strength of
the steel relative to the yield strength at 20◦C. The reduction of the yield strength
is divided into two types, the “effective” yield strength ky,θ with 2.0% strain and the
“design” yield strength kp,θ with 0.2% strain.
Reductions factors are given as a table for every 100◦C. Here linear interpolation
is allowed between points. The referred table is table (3.1) in (DS/EN 1993­1­2
+AC 2007, EC3­1­2). Figure 2.6a shows the reduction factor depending on steel
temperature.
(a) Eurocode reduction

in equation (2.15).

2.4
Design methods
When designing structural members, certain criteria must be considered accord­
ing to Eurocode (DS/EN 1993­1­1 +AC 2007) and (DS/EN 1993­1­2 +AC 2007).
The criteria depend on various situations that possibly can occur in which the steel
member is loaded. The following limit state designs are considered:
• ULS, Ultimate limit state
Associated with collapse or failure of the structure. The maximum load of
self­ and imposed loads is applied.
• ALS, Accident limit state
Associated with threat of serious injury, loss of life, and damage to the struc­
ture. Loads considered in case of various accidents; earthquake, vehicle
collision, and fire.
Other limit states as Serviceability limit state (SLS) and fatigue limit state (FLS)
are not considered in this thesis. The serviceability limit state (SLS) has been
excluded as the ULS and ALS design of the steel element will be done simultane­
ously. As the aim of the thesis is to optimize based on steel members, the criteria
of beams and columns are described in the following sections. Cross­section
class 4 is not considered in any calculations.
2.4.1
Beams
All calculations will consider a simple beam, assumed only subjected to bending.
It is also assumed that bending is the determining load case, however, shear
criterion will be taken into account. No design is done for lateral­torsional buckling
of beams.
ULS
Bending
The design bending moment resistance about one axis is:

2.5
Load combinations
The load on structures depends on the limit state. In this thesis, only ULS and ALS
will be considered as mentioned at the start of section 2.4. For both limit states,
different safety factors are used to ensure sufficient safety based on statistics. The
different safety factors are usually referred to as partial factors and are listed in
(DS/EN 1993­1­1 +AC 2007) and (Teknisk Ståbi, udgave 24. 2016). The safety
factors used for the case study are taken from (Teknisk Ståbi, udgave 24. 2016).
The load combinations used in this thesis is presented in the following. As the
building is in consequence class 2 the loads will be modified by a factor KFI = 1.
If the dead weight of the element is favorable the load combination shall use the
factor γG = 1.0. If the dead load is unfavorable γG = 1.2.
Sd,ULS = γG · Gk + γQ,1 · Qk,1 + γQ,2 · ψ0,2 · Qk,2 + γQ,n · ψ0,n · Qk,n
Sd,ALS = Gk + Ad + ψ2,1 · Qk,1 + ψ2,n · Qk,n
Wind and snow load will not be considered in this thesis as it is not considered in
the original car park design of (Joyeux, 2002). This car park is used for a case

2.6
Fire safety design and cost
Since the overall goal of this study is to optimize the combined price of steel and
insulation, the largest source of error may be the actual price. Prices for steel and
insulation vary widely. The price of steel depends on the amount of material used
and fabrication. A structure with large steel profiles will have a smaller steel kg
price than a structure with small steel profiles. The price of insulation varies in
terms of the chosen insulation product. Fire protection materials can be catego­
rized as dry or wet types. Mineral wool slabs are a dry type and are usually the
cheapest option while intumescent paint is a wet type and is the most expensive.
Despite prices of intumescent paint being the highest, it is also the most used fire
protection product according to (Steel Construction, 2022) as shown on figure 2.7.
Figure 2.7: Market share of fire protection materials from 2001 to 2017 (Steel
Construction, 2022).
The figures show a rise in popularity of intumescent paint over the past two decades.
This may be due to modern architectural design where intumescent paint often is
preferred as it gives the nicest finish to steel elements. The market size has also
increase with off­site applied intumescent paints.

Steel pricing
Steel is normally sold pr. kilogram. According to (Molio, 2022, Prisdata from
2022) finished steel is priced between 26 [kr/kg] ­ 45 [kr/kg] depending on the
amount of fabrication needed and amount of steel needed for the order. The price
includes material, fabrication, and surface treatment. However, due to the corona
pandemic, steel prices have skyrocketed with an increase of above 100% since
2019 (Sørensen, 2022).

2.6.2
Dry types of fire protection
Dry types of fire protection are usually applied directly onto the steel element in the
form of slabs, boards, or prefabricated sections. Dry types are often used when
the element is hidden from view. An advantage of most dry types is that they
can be applied to unpainted steelwork. When calculating the thickness of the
fire protection using SteFi, the thermal conductivity accordingly to (Pettersson, O.,
1976) is used.
Mineral wool slabs
Mineral wool slabs are mounted onto the structure by use of special glue, nails
or welding. It is normally classified as a soft insulation, but variations classified
as a hard insulation is also available. The thickness of the slabs is between 20
­ 50 [mm] and has a density of 150

2.6.3
Wet types of fire protection
Using wet types of fire protection has many advantages. It can be painted or
sprayed which is useful to cover complex shapes and details. The sprayed mate­
rials used are intumescent paint, sprayed mineral wool, and cementitious spray.
Only intumescent paint will be covered in this thesis as it is the most used product
and will be applied in the case study.
Intumescent paints
Intumescent paints or Intumescent coating is a fire protection product used to
protect load­bearing elements of buildings against fire. It is often used on elements
that shall be visible due to modern architectural design or tight spaces where other
options are not usable. It is mostly used for columns. ”An intumescent paint is a
coating that reacts to heat by swelling in a controlled manner to many times its
original thickness, producing a carbonaceous char formed by a large number of
small bubbles that act as an insulating layer to protect the substrate.” (Promat,
2018). This means that the insulating properties are highly dependent on the
ambient temperature. The thickness of the intumescent paints depends on tables
from suppliers which further depends on the section factor SF and the needed
duration or fire rating of the standard fire ISO834. The price of intumescent paints
varies depending on the supplier. To find a price that represents the market, the
price has been taken from Molios prisdata which is a database of pricing of work
and building for construction. Table 2.2 shows the price of intumescent paint from

3
Optimization
This chapter introduces optimization, the objective function for the thesis, bound­
ary conditions, and an analytical approach for structural optimization for elements
in fire.
3.1
General principle for optimization
Optimization is defined as ”an act, process, or methodology of making something
(such as a design, system, or decision) as fully perfect, functional, or effective
as possible” (Merriam­Webster, 2021).
One can also refer to optimization by
acquiring the best outcome under specific conditions. Structural optimization is
based on finding the best use of materials to comply with a given load. Finding
the optimum solution is an iterative process and the structural analysis is usually
conducted based on the finite element method (FEM). Structural analysis can be
combined with structural design to reduce cost and is often referred to as simulta­
neous analysis and design approach (Haftka and Gürdal, 1990). Generally, struc­
tural optimization can be divided into two broad categories, discrete or continuum
optimization. Continuum optimization is usually applied in topology optimization
while discrete optimization is used for size and shape optimization with predefined
or fixed topology (Mei and Q. Wang, 2021).
To obtain a solution for a structural optimization, one must select between three
basis geometric design parameterizations ­ size, shape and topology (Mei and Q.
Wang, 2021), (Christensen and Klarbring, 2009).
• Size optimization
Change of cross­sectional areas of structural members by changing design
variables such as thickness i.e. Also referred to as sizing optimization.
• Shape optimization
Change of form or contour of some part within the structural domain’s bound­
ary. Also referred to as configuration optimization.
• Topology optimization
Aiming to delete unnecessary structural members to achieve the optimal
design.
As the different profile types depend on the sections available in the building in­
dustry, the shape is predefined as discussed in section 2.1.1. The optimization
presented in this thesis will focus on one element in a structure at a time, hence
the topology of the total structure will not be taken into account. This leaves size
optimization to be the governing optimization process used in this thesis.
3.1.1
General mathematical form of a structural optimization
problem
A structural optimization problem also contains the following (Christensen and
Klarbring, 2009):
Automated and optimized design of steel structures in fire
23
==PAGE:36==
3.1. GENERAL PRINCIPLE FOR OPTIMIZATION
Chapter 3.
• Objective function (f)
A function used as a measure of the optimization result. In case of a mini­
mization optimization, the function f shall become as small as possible. The
function for this optimization problem is shown in section 3.2.
• Design variables (xi)
A function or vector that describes the design, and which can be changed
during optimization. The design variables for this optimization problem rep­
resent the geometry of the steel element.
• State variables (yi)
The state variables represent the response of the element. As the element
analyzed is a mechanical structure, the response means stress, strain, or
force.
A general mathematical form of a structural optimization problem can be written
as (Mei and Q. Wang, 2021):
Minimizef(x);
Subject to :

hj(x) refers to design constraints and equality constraints, gi(y) refers to behav­
ioral constraints. m and p are the numbers of constraints, and S is the search
space of the optimization problem. The constraints to the optimization problem
for this thesis can be found in section 3.2.1.
3.1.2
Mathematical formulation of multivariable optimization
Since the overall goal of this study is to optimize the combined price of steel and
insulation, the optimization function will have two variables, price of steel and price
of insulation. To find a minimum or maximum of a function with two variables, the
gradient vector of the function shall be determined. The gradient is the derivatives
of the function f(x, y). The gradient vector denotes the direction of the maximum
rate of change at any point. A general expression of the gradient is:
∇f(x, y) =
[∂f(x, y)
∂x
∂f(x, y)
∂y
]
To find the maximum or minimum of a function, known collectively as extrema,
stationary points must be determined. A stationary point (also known as critical
point) is where the derivative of f(x) is equal to 0. For a function with two variables,
both derivatives of f(x, y) shall equal 0. Setting the gradient equal to 0 and solving
for x and y gives all possible stationary points of the function f.
∇f(x, y) =
[∂f(x, y)
∂x
∂f(x, y)
∂y
]
= (0, 0)
Automated and optimized design of steel structures in fire
24
==PAGE:37==
3.1. GENERAL PRINCIPLE FOR OPTIMIZATION
Chapter 3.
To determine if the stationary point is a minima, maxima or a saddle point, the
Hessian matrix can be used. The Hessian matrix is a matrix of second order
partial derivatives.
Hf(x,y) =


∂2f(x, y)
∂x2
∂2f(x, y)
∂x∂y
∂2f(x, y)
∂x∂y
∂2f(x, y)
∂y2


The eigenvalues of the Hessian matrix can determine the classification of the sta­
tionary points. Suppose the function f(x, y) has continuous second partial deriva­
tives. Let (x0, y0) be a stationary point of f, and let λ1 and λ2 be the eigenvalues
of Hf(x0, y0) (Karsten Schmidt, 2018).
1. If λ1 < 0 and λ2 < 0 then f has a local maximum value at (x0, y0).
2. If λ1 < 0 and λ2 > 0 or if λ1 > 0 and λ2 < 0 then f has a saddle point at (x0, y0).
3. If λ1 > 0 and λ2 > 0 then f has a local minimum value at (x0, y0).
4. If either λ1 = 0 or λ2 = 0 (or both), then no conclusion can be drawn without
further investigation of the point.
For the optimization problem in this thesis, the statement regarding the local min­
imum value shall be satisfied.
3.1.3
Lagrange Multipliers
To satisfy constraints set by the boundary conditions, one must use Lagrange
Multipliers for optimization (Christensen and Klarbring, 2009). The Lagrange Mul­
tipliers technique lets you find the maximum or minimum of a multivariable function
f(x, y) when there is a constraint on the input values. The constraint function is
written as:
g(x, y) = c
It is possible to used multiple constraints for a function f(x, y). The core idea is to
find points where the gradient vectors of f and g are parallel to each other. The
Lagrange Multipliers technique use the above mathematics (gradient, Hessian
matrix, etc.) to solve the optimization problem. However, maximum or minimum
can be found at nonstationary points. To find the extreme values of f(x, y) subject
to a constraint g(x, y) = c, the Lagrange function must be defined:
L(x, y, λ) = f(x, y)−λ · [g(x, y)−c]
An extreme value must occur at a critical point of L(x, y, λ) = f(x, y). To inves­
tigate this, it is sufficient to solve the system of three variables x, y, λ given by
fx = λgx, fy = λgy, g(x, y) = c and then search among the resulting (x, y) to find
the minimum.
Automated and optimized design of steel structures in fire
25
==PAGE:38==
3.2. OPTIMIZATION FUNCTION
Chapter 3.
3.2
Optimization function
The optimization function aims to choose a steel profile with fire insulation that
complies with a set of boundary conditions, as well as having the lowest total
price. The boundary conditions shall ensure sufficient bearing capacity in ULS
and ALS.
The base option in the optimization function is the total price optimization as seen
in the equations (3.1). The total price is based on steel price Ps and the price of
insulation Pin. The steel price is based on the volume Vs of the steel multiplied with
steels density ρs and a unit steel price ps in [kr/kg]. The insulation price depends
on the exposed surface area As multiplied with the thickness din and unit price of
insulation pin in [kr/m2/m].
P = Ps + Pin
Ps = Vs · ρs · ps
(3.1)
Pin = Ap · din · pin
The total price of a fire­protected steel profile is by equation (3.2) equal to:
P = Vs · ρs · ps + Ap · din · pin
(3.2)
This is usually not easy to solve as the variables of volume, area, and insulation
thickness aren’t decoupled variables. This has previously been shown and high­
lighted by (Martin Thaarup, 2019). The solution is complex even with the volume
and area explained with two variables, while the rest is kept constant, hence not
a viable method.
For the optimization to be reliable, effective, and realistic, governing boundary
conditions for the static system as well as the geometry of the profiles shall be
determined. The boundary conditions for the static system are explained in the
following section 3.2.1. These boundaries are based on the explained theory in
chapter 2.
3.2.1
Boundary conditions for beams
Simple beams are usually designed for the given loads subjected to them, result­
ing in bending, shear, and torsion. In case of the optimization, it is assumed that
the beam is only subjected to bending, hence bending is the determining design.
Shear and torsion are not taken into account.
The first boundary condition BC 1), equation (3.4) ensures sufficient design bend­
ing moment resistance about one axis in ultimate limit state (ULS). The minimum
required section modulus Wmin to ensure sufficient capacity depends on the sec­
tion class, as shown in section 2.4.1.
The second boundary condition BC 2), equation (3.5) introduces the reduction
factor ξ for the yield strength fy, as this determines the bending moment resistance
in accidental limit states (ALS) according to section 2.4.1. The derivation of the
second boundary condition (BC 2) can be seen in appendix D. The reduction factor
ξ is given by two safety partial factor γM0 and γMfi, and a load combination factor
Automated and optimized design of steel structures in fire
26
==PAGE:39==
3.2. OPTIMIZATION FUNCTION
Chapter 3.
η. The load combination factor is based on the load combination in fire, divided
by the load combination at 20◦C. This can be expressed as:
η = γGA · Gk + ψ1 · Qk,1
γG · Gk + γQ,1 · Qk,1
(3.3)
The second boundary condition ensures the minimum bending moment capacity
in ALS at a minimum value of the reduction factor ξ. The reduction factor ξ corre­
lates with the critical temperature of the steel member, hence leading to the third
boundary condition.
The third boundary condition BC 3), equation (3.6) describes a function of the
maximum steel temperature correlated to the reduction factor ξ. This factor is
expressed by the section factor of the profile and the fire protection material prop­
erties. This ratio is referred to as the protected section. The section factor SF is
defined as the steel member encasement area per unit length Ap divided by the
volume of the steel member per unit length denoted Vs. Ap depends on the en­
casement area exposed to fire. The fire protection material properties are given
by the thermal conductivity λp and its thickness din. The thickness din is relevant
for the optimization function, hence the important parameter to determine based
on the third boundary condition.

3.2.2
Boundary conditions for columns
The bearing capacity of a simple column is determined as the load the column can
carry before global buckling occurs. The columns investigated in this thesis will
only be centric­loaded and no eccentricity will be taken into account. Columns of
section class 4 are not included in this thesis.
The first boundary condition BC 1), equation (3.7) ensures sufficient capacity about
two axes in ultimate limit state (ULS). Amin refers to the minimum required area
of the section. The capacity of a column depends on the column reduction factor
χ, which depends on the column length and section type.
The second boundary condition BC 2), equation (3.8) also introduces the reduc­
tion factor ξ for the yield strength fy, to determine the capacity in accidental limit
states (ALS). In ALS, the column reduction factor is changed to χfi which is a

3.3. ANALYTICAL FRAMEWORK FOR OPTIMIZATION
Chapter 3.
An attempt to solve the optimization problem analytically is preformed in the fol­
lowing sections.
3.3
Analytical framework for optimization
Fire protection for steel elements is usually done by numerical calculations. This
includes time­step iteration to determine the maximum steel temperature for a
given steel profile with a certain thickness of fire protection material. To create a
solvable analytical solution and use it as a general form of an optimization problem
is difficult due to two major challenges;
• Determining critical steel temperature
• Determining fire protection thickness
Determining these parameters is difficult to solve due to time­dependency. Heat
transfer equations for radiation and convection can be solved by a complex dif­
ferential equation related to time. The gas temperature is also time­dependent.
To solve the complex problem, it is simplified by linearising the temperature incre­
ments over small time steps, hence creating numerical time­step calculations.
Multiple attempts have been made to create analytical equations to determine the
critical steel temperature based on utilization, as well as prediction of insulation
from a given steel temperature. A fully combined analytical solution does not
yet exist. In the following section, determining the critical steel temperature and
prediction of insulation will be analyzed, discussed, and lastly combined to a full
analytical solution used for the optimization problem presented in section 3.2.
3.3.1
Prediction of critical temperature
Depending on the starting condition, the critical steel temperature is either a col­
lapse condition or a design criterion. As most engineers design using load condi­
tions, the critical steel temperature is determined based on the collapse condition:
pRd(Tcr) = pEd,fi
Ts,max < Tcr
Depending on the degradation model used, the temperature can be determined
based on the reduction factor. Using the degradation model according to Eu­
rocode [EC3­1­2] the temperature can easily be determined by linear interpolation.
A goal seek function would be needed to find the critical steel temperature using
Hertz’s material model, as it is a high­order polynomial, hence not useful as an
analytical solution. However, goal­seeking is a valid optimization strategy, and is
the only option when using Hertz’s material model.
Another option is the validation method starting with the fire and insulation proper­
ties. Then the critical temperature is set equal to the maximum steel temperature:
Ts,max = Tcr
pRd(Tcr) > pEd,fi
The reduction factor is calculated based on the chosen degradation model and
the load­bearing capacity is checked to ensure enough capacity. The method
however limits optimization options as it is a verification and not a design.
Automated and optimized design of steel structures in fire
29
==PAGE:42==
3.3. ANALYTICAL FRAMEWORK FOR OPTIMIZATION
Chapter 3.
Alternative critical temperature method
A third method depends on the utilization µ0 of the steel element in a fire situation.
This method is the simplest in terms of determining the fire resistance of a fire­
protected steel element. The expression is equation (4.22) in (DS/EN 1993­1­2
+AC 2007).
Tcr = 39.19ln
[
1
0.9674 · µ 3.833
0
−1
]
+ 482
[◦C]
However, this method is only valid for tension members and restrained beams.
This is due to the deformation criteria or stability considerations not being taken
into account. To be able to use this method, the utilization µ0 in fire must be known.
This is however not the case when optimizing. Using this method of calculating
critical temperature would most likely result in another boundary condition.
For analytical and optimization purposes, the goal­seeking method for determining
the critical steel temperature is the optimum choice.
3.3.2
Prediction of insulation thickness
The thickness of the fire protection material (also called insulation) is often found
by the spreadsheet method, which is based on time­step iteration, considering gas
temperature, steel temperature, thermal properties of the steel, and fire protection
material at a previous time interval. This results in calculations of the whole fire du­
ration, to find the critical time and required thickness of insulation. This method is
yet to be transformed into a simplified version, usable for analytical proposes. The
method is also limited by input values, resulting in re­calibration and re­calculation
of the scenario if the fire properties or insulation properties change. This highlights
that the spreadsheet method is time­consuming and not suitable to include in a
solvable analytical solution.
To create a solvable analytical solution for the thickness, there is a need for an
equation, leading back to equation (2.13). The equation can be simplified if ϕ = 0.
The fact that the specific heat of the protection material cp can be neglected, is
based on the following formula:
din · Ap · cp · ρp < ca · ρa · V
2
(3.10)
This method is given in (Jean­Marc and Real, 2010) and is commonly used for
light weight insulation materials. If the specific heat of the protection material cp is
neglected, it results to ϕ = 0 and equation (2.13) becomes:
∆θa,t = Ap

ϕ is calculated as in equation (2.14). Previous studies do not highlight an equation

attempts at nomograms evaluating the temperature in protected steel members
subjected to the standard fire curve ISO 834 are shown. This has previously
been done by (Jean­Marc and Real, 2010) to achieve a modified section factor.
The nomogram displayed by (Jean­Marc and Real, 2010) is steel temperature as
a function of the modified section factor, with either time or the modified section
factor as the x­axis. Figure 3.1 shows a nomogram with the modified section factor
as x­axis.
Figure 3.1: Nomogram showing steel temperature as a function of the modified
section factor for various times, based on ISO 834 curve (Jean­Marc and Real,
2010, Fig. 4.13).
Having the critical temperature of the steel and a resistance time, it is possible to
obtain the modified section factor, resulting in finding the insulation thickness din.
As the section factor can be calculated and the λp is given as a choice of fire
protection material, the thickness din can be calculated from the values of the
modified section factor.
Automated and optimized design of steel structures in fire
31
==PAGE:44==
3.3. ANALYTICAL FRAMEWORK FOR OPTIMIZATION
Chapter 3.
These nomogram curves are yet to be done for parametric fires, as changing one
of the three fire properties (opening factor O, thermal inertia b, or the fuel load q)
results in a new fire. To create similar nomograms for parametric fires, the three
fire properties must either be correlated or set to constant values. To minimize the
number of different nomograms, the ranges of the different fire properties can be
divided into reasonable tickmarks. The interval between the values of the opening
factor O would jump with 0.02 giving 10 values. The fuel load q would jump with
50, resulting in five values. The thermal inertia b will be set to 800 or 1160, as these
values represent a new well­insulated compartment and a concrete compartment.
If all fire properties were to be kept constant, it would result in roughly 10 opening
factor nomograms for each five fuel load values, times the two different thermal in­
ertia values, resulting in a total of 100 nomograms, which is not a reliable solution.
If the thermal inertia is to be kept constant and the opening factor is correlated
to the fuel load, the total number would be 10 nomograms, five for each value of
thermal inertia b. To simplify matters, the opening factor O will be limited to five
values.
An example of a nomogram for a parametric fire with a b­value of 800 J/m2s½K,
and a medium growing fuel load of 200 MJ/m2 is shown on figure 3.2.
Figure 3.2: Nomogram showing steel temperature as a function of the modified
section factor for various opening factors.
The data points in figure 3.2 have been calculated using the spreadsheet method,
Hertz’ material model, and a specific heat capacity for the steel depending on
the steel temperature. The steel density ρa is set to 7850 kg/m3. The thermal
resistance TR is calculated based on the required insulation thickness din and
the thermal conductivity of Vermiculite­based material by (Pettersson, O., 1976).
The section factor SF is set equal to 100 m−1.
To create an analytical equation to obtain the thickness of the protection material,

A 2nd order polynomial can be expressed as:
f(x) = a · x2 + b · x + c
(3.14)
To create a 2nd order polynomial which represents all the different opening factors,
the constants a, b & c must be expressed by the opening factor O. This is done by
fitting a power curve trendline to the different constants for each opening factor.
The power curve trendline can be expressed as:
f(x) = m1 · xm2
(3.15)

comparing the results with the nomograms as shown in figure 3.2. It is found to
be the negative solution that gives the correct solutions. The solution returns the
modified section factor.

Table 3.3 shows that the prediction model is within 6% of a full spreadsheet cal­
culation with various values of the variables O, q, T, and b. The main factor in the
difference is the specific heat of steel. As the temperature T varies, so does the
specific heat of steel, resulting in larger differences at low and high steel temper­
atures. The prediction for the model is best in the interval [200 ­ 550] ◦C. The
model could include various specific heat of steel values, but this would increase
the constants from 6 to 10, as C1 and C3 would become dependent of the specific
heat of steel. Due to this, the specific heat of steel has been kept constant at 600
J/kg K as the prediction is within 5% with steel temperatures in the range of [200 ­
550] ◦C. This range is considered useful as temperatures above 600◦C are rarely
used because the strength is heavily reduced.
Based on this test, the prediction model is assumed valid and can be used for an
analytical attempt at solving the optimization problem for a rectangular section.

3.3.4
Analytical attempt on solving optimization problem for
rectangular section
The solution to the analytical problem will be focused on the boundary conditions
for a beam. In the price function equation (3.2), the area and volume of the section
are included. As the price function should return a price in kr/m, the area and
volume inputs will change to perimeter and area of the section, as it is divided by
a length.
Automated and optimized design of steel structures in fire
36
==PAGE:49==
3.3. ANALYTICAL FRAMEWORK FOR OPTIMIZATION
Chapter 3.
For a rectangular section, the perimeter and area of the section can be expressed
as:
Ap = 2b + 2h
Vs = b · h
The height is expressed as h and the width as b. Using the expressions for the
perimeter and area, the section factor SF for a profile exposed to all four sides
can be expressed as:
SF = 2b + 2h
b · h
Boundary for bearing capacity in ULS
The choice of the profile must have a sufficient bearing capacity that is able to
carry a given load. The geometry of the profile is reflected in case of resistance
in the first moment of inertia of the cross­section. To ensure enough resistance
capacity, the section must uphold the first boundary condition referring equation
(3.4). The plastic moment for a rectangular section can be expressed as:
Wpl = b · h2
4
This can be rewritten to be expressed by the perimeter and area of the section,
which are inputs in the price function.
Wpl = Vs
Ap
· h · (b · h)
2
This is done to limit the number of variables included in the optimization. The first
boundary condition can now be rewritten into the following expression:
Vs
Ap
· h · (b · h)
2
≥MULS · γM0
fy
Boundary for bearing capacity in ALS
The second boundary condition ensures sufficient bearing capacity in ALS. The
capacity in fire depends on the temperature, as the reduction of the steel’s strength
ξ is dependent on the steel temperature.
By using the second boundary condition equation (3.5), the material reduction fac­
tor can be determined. From this, a material degradation model must be chosen,
which in this analytical attempt is the Hertz’s degradation model.
The critical temperature of the steel can then be determined and applied in the
third boundary condition.
Automated and optimized design of steel structures in fire
37
==PAGE:50==
3.3. ANALYTICAL FRAMEWORK FOR OPTIMIZATION
Chapter 3.
Boundary for insulation thickness
The amount of insulation material needed for a given profile depends on the size
and temperature of the profile. It is known that a profile with a large section factor
will require a thicker layer of insulation than a profile with a small section factor. It
is also known that the lower the critical steel temperature is, the more insulation
is required. The thickness of the insulation din is usually calculated by numerous
iterations or by programs, however it is yet to be determined by one analytical
formula.
By using the modified section factor given in equation (3.12), it is possible to deter­
mine the thickness of the insulation din. The value of the modified section factor
can be calculated using the solution to the 2nd order polynomial equation (3.18)
with the constants C1 −C6 chosen according to the fire properties. As the section
factor of the profile depends on the geometry, the thickness of the insulation din
also becomes dependent on the geometry, resulting in a simple equation:
din = SF · λp
f(O, T)
(3.19)
Optimization function
The price function can now be rewritten for optimization. First, the thickness of
insulation from the third boundary condition is substituted into equation (3.2):
P = Vs · ρs · ps + Ap · SF · λp
f(O, T) · pin
(3.20)
The volume, area, and section factor depends on the geometry variable b and h.
Substituting b and h into the equation gives a price function with two variables:
P(b, h) = b · h · ρs · ps + (2b + 2h)2 ·
λp
b · h · f(O, T) · pin
(3.21)
It is worth noting that the critical temperature from the second boundary condition
is a variable in the function for the modified section factor. The first boundary
condition is however not taken into account yet. It will be included at a later stage.
To find the minimum price, the gradient of the price function should be found and
equaled to zero. The gradient is dual to the total derivative, in this case, the
derivative of the function in respect to b and h.
As this function includes more than the two variables b and h, it would resolve in
a higher­order polynomial which result in a large number of solutions. To simplify
the function, constant values can be used for numerous variables.
A specific case with a set of given parameters can be analyzed.

An analytical solution to the optimization problem is possible using goal­seeking
and a prediction model for the thickness of insulation. Yet another constraint re­
garding the profile geometry is needed to ensure a realistic result. The equality
constraints g(y) was changed to a design constraints h(x) to simplify the calcula­
tion and keep the critical temperature constant. Otherwise, an analytical solution
to the optimization problem would be too difficult to calculate by hand. Another
restraint for the analytical solution is the limit of realistically available profiles. The
most common profiles as I­profile & RHS requires more constraint, hence a new
formulation of the optimization problem.
Automated and optimized design of steel structures in fire
44
==PAGE:57==
3.4. NUMERICAL FRAMEWORK FOR OPTIMIZATION
Chapter 3.
3.4
Numerical framework for optimization
Solving the optimization problem using numerical calculations is easier than an
analytical approach. Using computer­based methods, calculations that would be
time consuming can be reduced to seconds. Time­step iteration analysis is al­
ready integrated into the existing software SteFi. A goal­seek function to deter­
mine the critical steel temperature is also included, however, this shall be updated
to comply with the optimization calculations.
On the other hand, numerical calculations also have their limits. Using an an­
alytical approach, geometry can easily be kept as changing variables. Using a
numerical approach, calculations must be re­run to change any geometry. This
increases calculation time and changes the way an optimum solution is obtained.
A numerical calculation of a rectangular section with the same case as highlighted
in section 3.3.4 is performed.
The critical temperature is found by applying the second boundary condition (BC
2) in relation with the collapse condition method mentioned in section 3.3.1. This
leaves the critical steel temperature as a large one­dimensional vector ⃗Tcr, instead
of a constant value. The size of the vector ⃗Tcr depends on the profiles which com­
ply with the first and second boundary condition. The insulation thickness is then
determined based on the vector ⃗Tcr, geometrical properties and fire protection
properties. Without a limit to the width to height ratio b
h, the most optimized rect­
angular section is:
b = 0.02[m]
h = 0.56[m]
din = 12.2[mm]
p = 3574.55[kr/m]
ps = 3077.20[kr/m]
pin = 497.35[kr/m]
ξ = 0.593
Tcr = 566◦C
With the same limit to the width to height ratio b
h = 0.33, the most optimized rect­
angular section is:
Automated and optimized design of steel structures in fire
45
==PAGE:58==
3.4. NUMERICAL FRAMEWORK FOR OPTIMIZATION
Chapter 3.
b = 0.09[m]
h = 0.27[m]
din = 3.4[mm]
p = 6762.11[kr/m]
ps = 6676.43[kr/m]
pin = 85.68[kr/m]
ξ = 0.567
Tcr = 573◦C
The analytical and numerical yield similar results. The price of the numerical so­
lutions is larger by a tiny amount, due to the larger profile size. The amount of
insulation is lower for both cases, with or without the width to height ratio.
The main difference in the reduction factor ξ that depends on the critical steel
temperature for the different profiles. In the analytical solution, ξ is set equal to the
second boundary condition (BC2), resulting in solutions with a Tcr = 561◦C. This
leaves almost no room for optimization of the insulation thickness, as changing the
section factor SF is the only factor to influence the thickness while the rest are kept
constant. In the numerical solution, ξ is allowed to be a variable if it complies with
the second boundary condition. This gives room for optimization of the insulation
thickness. Another difference is how the insulation thickness is determined. The
analytical method use a prediction model based on the spreadsheet method, while
the numerical uses the spreadsheet method. This gives reason for the difference
in insulation thickness.
3.4.1
Verification of optimization included in SteFi

==PAGE:60==
4
Programming
The major upgrades and changes to SteFi will be explained in the following sec­
tions. The minor add­ons and changes will be listed with a short description.
The work and development of both the optimization calculation module and the
integration module into SteFi will be described by highlighting crucial implemen­
tation to SteFi. The entire source code for the new script Optimization.py can be
seen in the code report found in Appendix G.
4.1
Stefi
The existing software SteFi is a graphical user interface (GUI) with a purpose
to automate verification of steel elements in fire. By user input, the software can
calculate geometrical properties based on cross­section data. It can also calculate
load scenarios for beams and columns based on supports and loads, and create
both ISO834 and parametric design fire curves. Based on this, the software can
verify or design steel elements with fire protection insulation. For a fully in­depth
description of SteFis abilities, read (Mikkel Dohm Andersen and Thomas Dyhr,
2018, Automatic and BIM­Integrated Fire Design of Steel Elements).
4.2
Project files
SteFi is originally build on three separate Python files, a front­end file (SteFi.py),
a back­end file (BackEnd.py) and a report­file (Report.py). The front­end file runs
the graphical user interface (GUI) and is responsible for plotting graphs as well
as importing and exporting data. The back­end file is the main calculator, contain­
ing functions based on Structural fire design theory. The report file generates a
separate PDF file that highlights the calculations done by the user in SteFi. Ad­
ditionally, another back­end file (Optimization.py) is added. This file is used to
calculate the optimization of steel profiles based on input settings by the user.
The extra back­end calculation script creates a new code structure as shown in
figure 4.1.
Figure 4.1: Code structure and data flow.
Based on the actions called by the front­end file (SteFi.py), the different functions
in the three back­end files are executed. If a verification of steel elements is called;
Automated and optimized design of steel structures in fire
48
==PAGE:61==
4.3. OPTIMIZATION SCRIPT
Chapter 4.
the calculations will be done by the (BackEnd.py), while if an optimization is called;
the calculations will be done by the (Optimization.py). Figure 4.1 also shows new
import and export to Excel functions. These functions have been added for easier
saving and re­calculation using SteFi. This add­on will be described in section
4.5.
4.3
Optimization script
The optimization is written in a separate script called Optimization.py. The script
contains 9 functions. The purpose of the 9 functions will be described in the follow­
ing section. The function is created using if­statements, loops and the packages
xlrd (Excel import), Numpy (Data handling) and Panda (Data handling).
To obtain the optimum solution, SteFi must know the type of profile and its geom­
etry dimensions to calculate the optimization function. As mentioned earlier, size
optimization is the chosen optimization style. To accomplish the needs of SteFi,
a profile library shall be created.
• Profile library
A library of all mentioned profiles in section 2.1.1. Contains geometrical
dimensions.
To optimize based on a profile library, a library of the most common structural
steel profiles is created, containing geometrical dimensions. This library is created
in Excel, containing naming, geometrical dimensions, profile type, and welding
method.
The profiles included are from (Teknisk Ståbi, udgave 24. 2016, Teknisk Ståbi)
to represent the danish structural steel market and from (DS/EN 10365 2017),
(DS/EN 10210­2 2019), (DS/EN 10219­2 2019) & (EurocodeApplied, 2022) to
represent the international market. A simplified example is given below:

4.4
GUI
The interface of the optimization tab in SteFi is built in the same manner as the
remaining program. The GUI package Tkinter is an extension to Python and is
useful for creating interactable input, displaying text or value, etc. The previous
designers (Mikkel Dohm Andersen and Thomas Dyhr, 2018) have also used this
extension. By using Tkinter the interface tab for the optimization is as shown on
figure 4.2.
Automated and optimized design of steel structures in fire
53
==PAGE:66==
4.4. GUI
Chapter 4.
(a) optimization tab before inputs by user.
(b) Results displayed on optimization tab.
Figure 4.2: SteFi interface of optimization tab.
Before the user can access the optimization tab, he or she must perform a verifica­
tion of the originally chosen profile. The user must input cross­section data, loads,
boundary conditions, fire properties and choose deterioration model, stress type,
and calculation method. The reason for this is to be able to compare different
profiles to the original selection and to ensure that it is the same choices selected
for the optimization. When the user selects ”Design” in the Calculation tab, the
optimization tab as shown on figure 4.2a will become available. The GUI of the
optimization tab has been written in the front­end script (SteFi.py) as it is part of
the main program.
The tab is divided into four LabelFrames. Each frame represents widgets and text
labels. All settings on the optimization tab are based on ”declared variables”. De­
clared variables are elements that hold information about values or text. Variables
are used in functions, statements, or by other Tkinter modules (Radiobuttons, but­
tons, etc.). All text, values, and figures are saved as a variable. Variables used in
this project are:
• IntVar()
Holds an integer, E.g. 5
• DoubleVar()
Holds a float of a value, E.g. 5.4
• StringVar()
Holds a string (text), E.g. ”I­profile”
In the optimization tab, the user is informed of inputted data regarding cross­
section and insulation properties in the top right corner.
The same insulation
properties will be used for the optimized profile.
To use the optimization embedded in SteFi, the user must choose an optimization
Automated and optimized design of steel structures in fire
54
==PAGE:67==
4.4. GUI
Chapter 4.
style and setting. The optimization style depends on multiple user inputs/choices.
The user can in SteFi version 2.1.0 only select the option ”Profile library (TS)”.
The Radiobutton widget is chosen in this case, as it should be a toggle option.
Radio buttons can be assigned with a command, which calls a function or changes
a variable. Selecting the ”Profile library (TS)” brings up a new window shown on
figure 4.3.
Figure 4.3: Pop­up window of possible profiles to be selected for optimization.
The pop­up window shows the possible profiles that can be selected for use in the
optimization algorithm. To display the different options, the listbox widget is used.
This widget takes a list of strings as input and returns the selected choices. It is
possible to choose more than one option. If the user chooses a hollow profile, the
user must select the type of welding (Cold­formed or Hot­rolled) of the profile, as
the section modulus depends on this option. The dropdown menu is placed on
the optimization tab and is based on the optionmenu widget.
The user is now ready to input optimization settings. The user can input the price
of steel, price of insulation material, and the load combination factor η as seen
in figure 4.2a. This is done using the Entry widget, which is an interactive input
field. This gives the user the opportunity of changing input data. The input from
the Entry widget is then saved as a variable and can be used in calculations.
The optimization calculations can be initiated by clicking the ”Optimize” button,
supported by the Button widget. This widget is similar to the Radiobutton widget,
the main difference is that the button can be clicked and not toggled. The ”Opti­
mize” button runs functions from the Optimization.py and return an optimized steel
profile as displayed on figure 4.2b. The calculations of the steel profile and the
insulation thickness are based on inputs by the user in the previous tabs in SteFi.
Clicking the ”Optimize” button also activates the ”Plot optimization options” which
is a plot showing possible steel profiles with a lower total price than the original.
An example is given on figure 4.4.
Automated and optimized design of steel structures in fire

Chapter 4.
Figure 4.4: Plot showing profiles with lower price than the original selected.
The plot also shows the total cost in kr/m and the distribution between steel and
insulation cost by the colors blue and red.
The last button is labeled ”Print Report”. When selected, the report command runs,
as in the tab ”Calculation”. This button is included so the user can save and store
input and output data as nicely readable documentation. The report includes the
new optimized profile and its corresponding data, as well as the price calculation.
The report can only be created after the optimization has been completed and will
be saved as a .pdf file with a user­defined name at a chosen destination. The
report is generated by the package pdfgen from the Python module Reportlab.
The full addition code to the script SteFi.py can be found in appendix G.

4.5.1
Excel import & export
For an easier use of SteFi, import from and export to excel has been added. The
purpose of the import function is to quickly set all settings in SteFi to the desired
choice so the calculation of the steel profile can be performed much quicker. The
export function has been added to save geometry and calculation choices for later
use. Both the import and export are based on an excel file. The file is structured
to contain the name of the property, value, and unit. This is done to secure easy
readability and use. The columns and rows in excel file must not be changed, as
it would result in import errors. Figure 4.5 shows the import/export file.

To be able to add more functions to SteFi, code is added to the original scripts
(SteFi.py) and (Report.py). The GUI itself and extra optimization functions have
been added to the (SteFi.py) script to run the optimization from the GUI.
Some modifications have been done to the (Report.py) script to ensure that it
prints the correct data when called. The price of profiles has also been added
when the user clicks the ”Print Report” button in the Optimization tab. The extra
added code can be seen in appendix G.

4.6. COMPILING
Chapter 4.
4.6
Compiling
The further development of the program has been done using Python as it is a
free and open­source programming language, which can be run on mainstream
operating systems. To write code using Python, the software Spyder have been
used. Spyder is a free and open­source environment for advanced editing, analy­
sis, and debugging Python scripts (Team Spyder, 2022). After the code is finished
the scripts shall be combined and turned into a program. However, Spyder cannot
compile scripts into an executable file (.exe). Generally compiling Python code is
not a simple task compared to a code this is written in a different programming
language.
To create an updated version of SteFi, a Python extension called py2exe will be
used. py2exe converts Python scripts into executable Windows programs that
can run without the need of a Python installation. This limits the use of SteFi to
machines with windows as operating system, however the compile process of the
program is much simpler than the previous used extension PyInstaller (Mikkel
Dohm Andersen and Thomas Dyhr, 2018).
To create the application, a setup file is needed. The setup file includes a list of
packages that must be included in the program, naming, icon, and building folders.
The setup file can be viewed in appendix G. Compiling the code with py2exe
is done by running the following command in the windows terminal (command
prompt) after the directory has been changed to the current directory of the source
code:
1
python setup.py py2exe
A successful compile results in the following folders and file type shown on figure
4.6.
Figure 4.6: Folders and files created after a successful compile.
Automated and optimized design of steel structures in fire
58
==PAGE:71==
4.7. NOTES ON STEFI
Chapter 4.
4.7
Notes on SteFi
The software SteFi is still in development and must be used with caution. The pro­
gram is best used as a first draft for designing or verifying steel elements followed
up by proper documentation.
4.7.1
Cross-section class
The current version of SteFi does not consider the cross­section class when opti­
mizing, and due to this uses the plastic moment resistance for all profiles.Thereby,
it is important to check the cross­section class to ensure that the profile is class 1
& 2.
4.7.2
Hindered thermal expansion
The software contains a calculation that is advised not to be used in combination
with the optimization. The previous version of SteFi ­ 2.0.0 includes calculation
of columns with hindered thermal expansion based on the work of (Inge­Mette
Kjemtrup, 2021, Inge­Mette Kjemtrup). This part has been excluded from the
optimization module in version SteFi ­ 2.1.0 ­ Beta as it has not been tested and
verified. SteFi ­ 2.1.0 ­ Beta is still able to calculate columns with hindered thermal
expansion when used without the optimization algorithm. This has been tested
and verified by the author in a previous study (Nikolaj Norheim Hansen, 2021).
Automated and optimized design of steel structures in fire
59
==PAGE:72==
5
Case study
This case study will serve as an example of the optimization approach presented
in this report. The following sections describe a case study on a car park and are
based on a demonstration of a real fire test in an open car park. The geometry of
the car park is taken from (Joyeux, 2002, EUR 20466). The price of the car park
will be based only on structural steel and fire protection. Third prices of the car
park will be compared. ”Non­protected design” based on the local fire described
in (Joyeux, 2002, EUR 20466), ”Protected design” based on a parametric fire cor­
responding to car park fire, and ”Optimized design” based on the same parametric
fire with optimization of profiles and insulation.
A real experimental case of the car park was performed and documented in (Joyeux,
2002). The demonstration was performed to determine multiple objects: being the
rate of heat release of cars, the temperature of the structural elements, and fire
behavior of parts of the structure. The car park was designed based on experi­
mental results from the technical report (Joyeux, 2002) which predicted a maxi­
mum steel temperature of more than 900◦C. The experimental test­1 showed that
the maximum steel temperature for the lower flange of one of the beams was ap­
proximately 700◦C. The maximum steel temperature of an exposed flange of the
column was 640◦C. The maximum ambient temperature was above 1000◦C while
the maximum average ambient temperature was 500◦C. This indicated that the
fire was intensive.
5.1
Description
Figure 5.1: Isometric view of the car park.
The case study chosen is a 1­story car park. The building is 3 m high to the
highest floor plan (level­1). The building is 32 m long and 15 m wide. The car park
Automated and optimized design of steel structures in fire
60
==PAGE:73==
5.2. STATIC SYSTEM
Chapter 5.
is defined as an open car park. At the level of each floor are expected 24 parking
areas, the total capacity of the car park building is approximately 48 parking areas.
The connection between the floors is an external two­way ramp. Ventilation of the
building is natural as the sides of the car park are open with a metal fence for
safety.
5.2
Static system
The building consists of a steel beam/column system and a concrete floor slab
spanning on top of the steel beams. Each concrete slab spans 2.5 m. The slab is
a composite slab made by a steel sheet Cofrastra 40 and a concrete slab with a
thickness of 120 mm. The slab is reinforced with ø16 rebars in both directions for
global stability and in case of fire. The static system for the car park can be seen
in figure 5.2.
Figure 5.2: Isometric view of the static system of the car park.
The primary system is the columns on lines A, B & C, beams in lines A, B & C,
and in lines 1­4. The concrete deck and the remaining beams spanning between
lines A, B & C is the secondary system (figure 5.3). The vertical load is transferred
via the concrete decking to the supporting steel beams, which is supported by the
steel columns on each floor. The horizontal load is transferred from the concrete
decks to the steel beams, then to the columns and bracing systems, which transfer
the load to the foundations. The load in the foundation and the bracing systems
will not be taken into account.

5.3
Non-protected design
A design using the local design fire has not been performed in this thesis. However,

insulation is applied to any beams or columns. The car park is designed based
on the local fire and experimental results from the technical report (Joyeux, 2002)
which found a maximum steel temperature of approximately 700◦C. According
to the reduction factor from the Eurocode, the critical steel element would have
a reduced capacity of 23% of the yield strength if considering 2% strain. The
columns in the reference design case of the car park is not considered.

The car park in this protected design case is based on a parametric fire. The input
