==PAGE:77==
5.4. PROTECTED DESIGN
Chapter 5.
5.4.1
Assumptions
As a parametric fire affects the entire car park at once, it is assumed that the
temperature of a beam will be uniformly distributed. The beams is exposed to fire
on three sides as the top side is covered by the concrete slab. It is assumed that
the structural integrity of the car park is not compromised by using the 2% strain
“effective” yield limit nor is the intumescent paint. The self­weight of the beams in
lines A, B & C will not be considered for simplicity.
The price of steel and intumescent paint is set accordingly to section 2.6.
5.4.2
Fire properties
The maximum gas temperature of the parametric fire is calculated using the theory
presented in section 2.2.2. Inputs for the parametric fire is as follows:
• Fuel load q 300 MJ/m2
• Opening factor O 0.20 m−1
• Thermal inertia b = 897 J/(m2s½K)
The fuel load is set to 300
[
MJ/m2]
. This value is taken as an average value based
on suggested values in (Hertz, K.D., 2021) and (Spearpoint et al., 2015).(Hertz,
K.D., 2021) highlights that the densest parking buildings results in a fuel load of
330
[
MJ/m2]
while a fuel load of 260
[
MJ/m2]
would suffice for more spacious car
parks. If the fuel load was based on category 4­5 cars from table 3.1 in (Joyeux,
2002) with a fire load of 12 GJ, then the fuel load would be 232 MJ/m2. This study
is however from 2002 and does not consider new modern cars. Large electric car
represents a fire load of 15 GJ according to (Hertz, K.D., 2021). Most studies on
car parks also used a fuel load in the range set to 250 −350
[
MJ/m2]
.
The opening factor is set equal to the maximum limit of 0.20 m−1. The opening
factor is calculated to be 0.21 m−1 with the conservative assumption that 1 m of
the wall opening is closed off. This assumption is based on the fact that the cars
parked close to each other shield movement of the air in the bottom part and leave
free movement of air only above the cars. As the car park is open and relatively
small for a car parking building, the value of the opening factor seems reasonable.
The thermal inertia b for the structure is set equal to to 1160 J/(m2s½K). This value
is selected based on (Hertz, K.D., 2021). However, a more realistic representation
of the thermal inertia is done by multiplying with an area percentage related to
the area of the floors divided by the total area of the compartment. This gives a
thermal inertia of 897 J/(m2s½K).
The opening factor and thermal inertia are not easily determined for a car park as
it is considerably different from a normal compartment to which the theory of the
parametric fire apply. However, the found values are acceptable estimates for the
design fire.

geometry. The design of the beams are according to (DS/EN 1993­1­1 +AC 2007)
and (Pettersson, O., 1976) using (Kristian Dahl Hertz, 2019) reduction factor. The

profile might change the height of the structure. Cost of connections are also not

an IPE550 is high and not many standard profiles exist with the same capacity.

5.6
Comparison of design
The different designs of the car park is shown in figure 5.7.
Figure 5.7: Comparison of cost distribution on the left y­axis and resistance time
on the right y­axis for the different car park designs.
The figure shows that both parametric fire design yields a higher cost for the
beams. The protected design using the parametric fire shows an increase in the
price of 33%. The optimized design using the parametric fire shows an increase in
the price of 26%. The resistance time for car parks is also a governing requirement.
The resistance time for car parks vary greatly from country to country. Figure 5.7
clearly shows the improved fire safety level of the protected cases. The local fire
only has a equivalent resistance time of 15min., while the equivalent resistance
time of the protected designs is three times better (47 min. and 46 min.), hence
31 minutes longer. The increase in cost is expected as the fire safety level has
risen. Added fire safety for the building is usually a good selling point, however,
it depends on the cost. An insurance company would most­likely see a benefit in
more safety; however, a risk analysis might show that the increase in the price of
26% does not cope with the increased safety. Another aspect is durability. As the
Eurocode states, car parks should be designed to prevent collapse. A car park
designed using a local fire design should have a potentially larger loss of income
if damaged than a parametric designed car park. One could argue that a car park
designed using a local fire is not fit to be repaired.
Automated and optimized design of steel structures in fire
68
==PAGE:81==
5.6. COMPARISON OF DESIGN
Chapter 5.
The design of this car park is from 2002. A Recent study (Hertz, K.D., 2021)
highlights a need for revisiting the methods for local design fires. This is based
on:
• Outdated values of the fire load given by a burning car
• Local heating scenarios, where cold adjacent elements contribute to the fire
resistance of the heated element
• Use of ultimate strength instead of yield strength for the steel resistance
In the light of this study, the non­protected design should have been reanalyzed to
ensure the design of the car park. An additional aspect that is worth noticing is that
the utilization factor for the reference case with IPE elements which is relatively
low, indicating that the design might not have been the most optimum in ULS.
However, one could argue that this is due to the SLS limit state which has not
been checked. If the utilization factor were close to 100% for all profiles, there
would be no cheaper options when using larger standard profiles for this case
study.
If the optimized design is compared to the protected design, it is only 5% cheaper,
highlighting minimal optimization. If the secondary beams (IPE550 of the origi­
nal design) that represent 91% of the total cost, were to be fully optimized with­
out using standard profiles, a possible larger saving could be found. However, a
RHS460x300x10 which is the minimum RHS with enough resistance would only
lead to an optimization of 7% cheaper than the protected design, hence only an
increase of 2%.
Other possible reasons for the low optimization saving are:
• The section factor SF
• The high opening factor O because it is an open car park
• Using the design yield strength with a strain of 0.2%.
If the beams were exposed to fire on all four sides, it would result in a higher
section factor SF. A higher section factor leads to more intumescent paint, as the
critical temperature of the steel element would rise. The perimeter of the section
would also increase, requiring additional fire protection. This is one of the reasons
optimization of columns is important. A lower opening factor would result in a
longer fire, hence more heating of the steel. This would also result in more fire
protection and possibly more savings. Finally, calculating the beams with a strain
of 0.2% would also require more fire protection for the steel element. One could
argue that the structural integrity of the car park is compromised by using the 2%
strain “effective” yield. The intumescent paint could also be damaged.
A final effort has been done to determine when the optimization returns a profile
with a larger capacity compared to the original choice. A utilization factor close
to 100% has been opted for. A protected and an optimized design is shown on
figure 5.8. The calculations can be found in appendix F.
Automated and optimized design of steel structures in fire
69
==PAGE:82==
5.6. COMPARISON OF DESIGN
Chapter 5.
(a) IPE550 with fire protection.
(b) RHS500x300x12.5 with fire protection.
Figure 5.8: Distribution of cost in % of different designs.
The figure shows that a design where the cost of the fire protection is equal to
or larger than the cost of steel can benefit from optimized design with a larger
profile. The cost of the fire protection is in this case reduced by 60%, while the
cost of steel is increased with 43%. As the steel is cheaper than the paint, this
optimization leads to a saving of 11%. The cost ratio for steel and fire protection
where a larger profile is beneficial cannot be determined based on this case study,
however it can be argued that a higher need of fire protection results in better
optimization.
Automated and optimized design of steel structures in fire
70
==PAGE:83==
6
Conclusion
Steel structures are usually optimized to carry the mechanical loads and comply
with maximum allowed displacements using the minimum amount of steel. This
method keeps the steel cost to a minimum but does not consider the accidental
limit state regarding fire verification. This often results in high costs regarding
insulation of the structure, neutralizing the original steel optimization. To address
this problem, an optimization algorithm is developed that optimizes the total cost
of steel and insulation.
In this thesis, three main parts regarding the optimization algorithm have been
conducted; Analytical framework, implementation of optimization in SteFi and a
case study. These are achieved by applying optimization and structural fire design
theory, as well as creating boundary conditions based on verification of structural
elements according to the Eurocode. The potential of optimization regarding steel
elements with fire protection is shown in a case study.
The conclusion to this thesis is split into three sections, the first part represents
the analytical framework, the second part, the implementation of optimization in
SteFi and the third part an exemplified optimization using a case study.
6.1
Analytical framework
The analytical framework is created based on optimization theory, structural de­
sign of steel elements according to the Eurocode, and design fires. This section
includes boundary conditions, prediction of insulation thickness, and an analytical
solution.
a) According to optimization theory, one must determine the boundaries of the
problem. The Boundary conditions regarding resistance are divided into
beams and columns.
• For beams, it is found possible to determine a minimum value of the
reduction factor ξ using the load combination factor η.
• For columns, the second boundary condition seems to cause mislead­
ing results. This appears to be because the reduction factor ξ is used
to determine both the capacity and reduction in fire χfi.
b) The critical steel temperature is normally determined based on the collapse
condition using the utilization factor µ at 20 ◦C or the reduction factor ξ.
• For analytical purposes, the critical steel temperature must be deter­
mined by the reduction factor ξ to limit parameters.
• The best method is concluded to be goal­seeking as discussed in sec­
tion 3.3.1, as Hertz’s material model is irreversible. However, this method
is more a numerical approach than it is analytical.
Automated and optimized design of steel structures in fire
71
==PAGE:84==
6.2. IMPLEMENTATION OF OPTIMIZATION IN STEFI
Chapter 6.
c) The main challenge of an analytical solution to determine insulation thick­
ness is the fact that the steel temperature is obtained by time discretization
of the heating equation. The insulation thickness depends on the gas tem­
perature, steel temperature, thermal properties of the steel, and fire protec­
tion material. For analytical purposes, a simpler method is needed.
• Section 3.3.2 presents a function of the variables; Opening factor O
and steel temperature T. This function can be used to predict a value
of the modified section factor for parametric fires. The modified section
factor is a ratio between the section factor and the thermal resistance
of the fire protection.
• The function is validated using different cases of thermal inertia b, open­
ing factor O, fuel load q, and steel temperature T compared to full
spreadsheet calculation. The results of insulation thickness were within
6 %, concluding that the prediction function is a valid calculation method.
However, the results were underestimating the thickness.
• The function is limited to steel temperatures in the range of [200 ­ 550]
◦C due to the results from the validation.
• The model also follows the ”rule” for equivalent fires, but there is no
correlation in the constants C1 −C6. It can also be concluded that the
6 constants C1 −C6 depend on the thermal inertia b, fuel load q, and
the specific heat of steel cp.
d) An attempt on solving an optimization problem for a rectangular section high­
lighted the best possible options for the width b and height h.
• The solution is validated by using the Lagrange Multipliers technique,
numerical and graphical representation.
• A constraint regarding the profile geometry must be introduced if the
optimized profile shall be realistic.
6.2
Implementation of optimization in SteFi
This thesis presents the development and test of an optimization module, based
on optimization theory. An optimization module has been achieved and included in
the existing software SteFi. The module can highlight an optimal profile choice for
a given initial design using numerical methods written in Python. A profile library
has been added to the BackEnd of SteFi for optimization purposes. A graphical
representation in the program is available to present the optimization results for a
quicker overview of possible saving options. An additional feature added to SteFi
is the option to import and export input data. The performance of the optimization
module has been verified by a small case study in section 3.4.1 and a larger case
study in section 5. To ensure the validity of the results of the optimization module,
external use of the optimization should be done before it is used in a real­life
application. This is the reason why SteFi is kept in a beta version 2.1.0.
Automated and optimized design of steel structures in fire
72
==PAGE:85==
6.3. CASE STUDY
Chapter 6.
6.3
Case study
The case study is included to investigate the possible savings of optimizing a car
park designed with a parametric fire as well as determining parameters that affect
the optimization. The optimization only covers the beams in the structure.
• It can be concluded that a protected design of a car park can be optimized
using standard profiles with a saving of 5%. Using non­standard profiles, a
saving of 7% is possible for the beams.
• The case study also highlights a saving of 11% on the steel beams compared
to a normal protected design if the cost of the fire protection is equal to or
larger than the cost of steel.
• Only optimizing on beams is not realistic. Higher optimization savings are
expected for columns as more insulation is required than for a beam.
• An optimized car park designed using a parametric fire with a higher fire
safety level increases the cost by 26% for the steel beams compared to a
local fire design.
• The equivalent resistance time for the protected designs is increased by 31
minutes compared to the non­protected design. The increase in resistance
time highlights the added safety level.
• The influence of the fire properties for the parametric fire shows a large
difference in savings. More case studies must be conducted to investigate
when optimization is relevant, and when it is too time­consuming.
• Only intumescent paint has been used for fire protection. A study on how the
cheaper fire protection materials affect the saving is still a question. More
testing using the optimization module could highlight good and bad optimiza­
tion cases.
• The cost of connections will not be included. However, the price of connec­
tions might vary depending on the different profile types. A further study
investigating the cost of connections for different profiles could be used to
analyze if an extra price would equalize the optimization saving found by
change of profile type.
6.4
Limitations and further studies
This thesis has covered the analytical framework and implementation of optimiza­
tion by programming, all including limitations and further studies. The following
sections list these.
6.4.1
Justification of case study
The case study only optimizes the beams. The saving is minimal as the insulation
thickness needed for the profiles is low. If columns were included, it would be
expected that the saving would be higher because more insulation is required for
columns than beams. Without including columns, the case study is unrealistic.
Automated and optimized design of steel structures in fire
73
==PAGE:86==
6.4. LIMITATIONS AND FURTHER STUDIES
Chapter 6.
One should also consider the serviceability limit state (SLS) as well as the cross­
section class. A saving of only 5% is likely not considered large enough to change
the design.
If the beams required more insulation thickness due to a more severe parametric
fire, the possible saving is raised to 11%. This is because the cost of insulation
equals the cost of steel. Using optimization, the cost of the fire protection can be
reduced by 60%, while the cost of steel only is increased by 43%, leading to the
saving of 11%. This highlights that the more fire protection needed, the better is
the optimization working.
6.4.2
Effective thermal conductivity of intumescent paint
Intumescent paint is the most used fire protection material. Due to this, it is also
the chosen fire protection for the case study. However, the thermal conductivity is
set to a constant value in this thesis. Further studies could analyze the change of
thermal conductivity and verify if the constant value used is a good assumption.
The effective thermal conductivity presented in this thesis is based on different
resistance times to the standard fire. A study is missing of the required nominal
insulation thickness for parametric fires.
6.4.3
Modified section factor
This master thesis has covered a function for predicting the required modified sec­
tion factor which is a ratio between the section factor and thermal resistance of
the fire protection. The modified section factor is useful for determining the thick­
ness of the insulation. The function is limited to parametric fires and by tabulated
constants C1 −C6. The constants are only valid for specific values of thermal
inertia b, fuel load q, and specific heat of steel cp. In case of heavy insulation ma­
terial, where ϕ ̸= 0, the modified section factor should according to (Jean­Marc
and Real, 2010) be corrected. However, what defines a heavy insulation material
is not stated by (Jean­Marc and Real, 2010), hence the limitation is unknown.
Further study of the constants C1−C6 could highlight the dependency of variables.
Another study could clarify if linear interpolation between values of modified sec­
tion factor is acceptable.
6.4.4
Implementation of more advanced optimization
Implementing the optimization process in SteFi is time consuming due to program­
ming. Because of time and difficult development, certain features could not be
implemented. A useful feature to be implemented is the option to optimize based
on variables and limit a variable (height h, width b, or thickness t) of a section.
Another case to consider is an optimization of elements subjected to the combina­
tion of compression and bending. To be able to achieve the previous mentioned
optimization, optimization of columns must be solved. Then one could focus on
columns with the effect of hindered thermal expansion followed up by verification
and optimization of elements in bending and compression. Another study could
shine some light on beams with hindered thermal expansion to address which
reduction factor of 0.2% or 2.0% shall be used in which cases.
Automated and optimized design of steel structures in fire
74

den ubeklædte Conlit 150 sikres ved en let sprøjtemaling med plastmaling.

ROCKWOOL Conlit 150 P er et formfast og stift
produkt med overordentlige gode
brandbeskyttende egenskaber, der anvendes til
brandisolering af bærende stålkonstruktioner og
til brandlukninger. Conlit 150 P ubeklædt, men
fås også belagt med armeret alufolie eller hvid
vlies på den ene side.

PROMAPAINT®-SC4 Brandmaling            Produktdatablad    B 81.0  
 
 
Anvendelse: 
● Bjælker/søjler-stål 
 
Beskrivelse 
PROMAPAINT®-SC 4 er en fiberfri hvid maling til indvendig og 
semieksponeret brug på bærende stålkonstruktioner. 
Produktet bruges på hule og åbne stålprofiler, der kræver fra 
15 – 120 minutters brandbeskyttelse. Produktet påføres med 
airless sprøjte og på mindre områder med pensel.  
Primer kompatibel med PROMAPAINT® -SC4*: 
● Akrylbaseret (max 0,03-5 mm filmtykkelse)  
● Alkydbaseret (max 0,03-5 mm filmtykkelse)  
● 2-komponent epoxy opløsningsmiddelbaseret  
● 2-komponent epoxy vandbaseret  
● Zinkmættet epoxy (80/96 masseprocent metallisk zinkpulver) 
● Zinksilikat  
● Polybutadien (Promat® TY-ROX) 
Topmaling kompatibel med PROMAPAINT® -SC4*, ** 
Tilsigtede anvendelser Z2,Z1,Y & X: 
● Interthane 990 (2-komponent polyuretanakryl) 
● Temathane 50 (2-komponent polyuretan) 
● UT 6581(2-komponent polyuretan) 
Tilsigtede anvendelser Z2,Z1 & Y: 
● LATEXOR (copolymer akryl) 
● TEKNODUR 050 (polyuretan) 
● Polistrya 01(polyuretanakryl) 
● PRIMODUX H (epoxy) 
● Redox PUR Finish Gloss (2-komponent aliphatic polyuretan) 
● S 2071(uretanalkyd) 
● Rubbol XDGloss (aphftagh solid opløsningsbaseret) 
● Challenger Hi-Tech (polyuretan) 
● HEMPATHANE TOPCOAT 55210 (2-komponent Glossy 
polyuretanakryl)

Side 2 af 39 
 
 
 
PROMAPAINT®-SC4 Brandmaling            Produktdatablad    B 81.0  
*Den samlede filmtykkelse (TFT) for primer og topmaling bør ikke 
overstige 0,2 mm. Kontakt altid leverandøren af primer/topmaling for 
endelig anvisning og råd. 
**Filmtykkelse iht. producenten af topmalingen. Ved anvendelse af 
generisk topmaling, må der max påføres 0,09 mm lagtykkelse. 
PROMAPAINT® -SC4 er godkendt for anvendelse på underlag 
af galvaniseret stål med lagtykkelse af zink coating på ≤ 0,2 
mm.  
Malingssystem bestående af 2-komponent epoxy 
opløsningsmiddelbaseret primer, PROMAPAINT® -SC4, og 
Carbothane 134 PU topmaling opfylder kravene for 
korrosionsklasse C3 iht. EN ISO 12944-2.   
Ordliste 
VFT: Våd Film Tykkelse, den filmtykkelse der kan måles med 
vådfilmkam. Omregnes til TFT ved at gange med 0,6. 
Fx.: 0,30 mm VFT  gange 0,6 = 0,18 mm TFT. 
NTFT: Nominel Tør Film Tykkelse, den min. filmtykkelse 
nævnt i tabel 1 – 36. 
TFT: Tør Film Tykkelse, den faktiske filmtykkelse der påføres 
på stålet. TFT skal være ≥ NTFT.  
Tabeller 1 - 36, generelt 
● viser filmtykkelsen (NTFT) af PROMAPAINT®-SC 4 (excl 
primer og topcoat) iht. EN 13381-8. 
● stålprofiler sandblæst til ISO 8501-1 SA2½ o.l. og påført de 
nævnte primere og topmaling. 
● for kritisk ståltemperaturer fra 350° C til 750° C. 
Tabeller 1 – 7 (ETA 13/0198):  
● gældende for ’H’, ’I’, ’U’, ’L’, ’T’,’Z’ formede åbne stålprofiler, 
ved 3-sidet brandpåvirkning af bjælker og 4-sidet 
brandpåvirkning af søjler.  
● brandmodstandsevne R15 til R120. 
Tabeller 8 – 13 (WF362028):     
● gældende for ’H’ & ’I’formede åbne stålprofiler, ved 3-sidet 
brandpåvirkning af bjælker. 
● brandmodstandsevne R15 til R90. 
Tabeller 14 – 19 (WF362028):     
● gældende for ’H’ & ’I’formede åbne stålprofiler, ved 4-sidet 
brandpåvirkning af bjælker med NFTF ≤ 2,473 mm og 4-
sidet brandpåvirkning af søjler. 
● brandmodstandsevne R15 til R90. 
Eksempel på dimensionering af filmtykkelse: 
Konstruktion: 
        HEB 140 stålsøjle 
        4-sidet brandbeskyttelse 
Forudsætninger: 
 Kritisk ståltemperatur 500° C 
 Indvendig montering i tørt miljø (Z2). 
 60 min. brandmodstandsevne 
Dimensionering: 
Find stålprofilets µ/A forhold (sektionsfaktor): 
µ = indvendig omkreds af stålprofilet:4*b + 2*h (se Figur 1) = 
4*0,14 + 2*0,14 m = 0,84 m. 
A = altid hele stålprofilets tværsnitsareal, se Teknisk Ståbi = 
4,30 mm2 x 10-3 ≈ 0,0043 m2. 
µ/A = 0,84 m / 0,0043 m2 = 195,36 m-1 ≈ 196 m-1. 
Filmtykkelse (NTFT) PROMAPAINT®-SC 4,tabel 5 =1,374 
mm. 
          
               
 
     
            
NB: uanset brandbeskyttelse er det altid hele stålprofilets 
tværsnitsareal (A) der skal bruges til dimensionering.

Cofrastra® 40 is a steel profile sheet with dovetail geometry 
intended to realise composite slabs
The longitudinal shear bond between the concrete and the 
profile realised by embossment and its dovetail section gives 
an additional reinforcement to the slab construction. 
The profile serves as formwork while pouring the concrete and 
allows savings to be made on the lower reinforcement layer.
Cofrastra® 40 permits to build very slim and lightweight slabs, 
or quite massive structures, similar to standard reinforced 
concrete slabs using it more as lost shuttering. 
Its dovetail geometry ensures a very good adhesion and the 
Cofrafix clip system allows to suspend building equipment, 
ducts or false ceilings … without any dowels, pins or drilling.

According EN1994-1-2 4.3.2, Cofrastra® 40 composite floors are by default REI 30. 
For higher fire resistance classes, reinforcement bars are required. These are positioned in the ribs of the profile. Their size is determined by 
calculation (see Cofra5).
The acoustic behaviour of a raw composite slab is determined by its mass.
Given values are calculated by modelling – study report CSTB No. AC15-26054708
REI: fire protection rating of the raw composite slab 
The minimum thickness is required to comply with the 
temperature criterion (I) on the non- fire exposed side.

Technical University of Denmark

Date    11 December 2020            
11023 and 11B01 STRUCTURAL FIRE SAFETY DESIGN
Modified for case study
Fire properties has been changed from original. 
Fig.1:  static scheme of the beam (left) and cross-section of the beam profile (right)

Problem 1 (WRITTEN) 
The steel beam in Fig.1 must be insulated against fire, using vermiculite-based board panels of conductivity 
given in Tab.1 and knowing that:  
‐ The beam is simply supported, has a length L = 5 m and carries a uniformly distributed load in fire equal 
to p = 70 kN/m. Please note that the load value given is the total design load for the fire case, where 
reduction coefficients for the fire condition have already been applied to the characteristic values of the 
permanent and live loads. 
-
The beam is made of steel ST42 with characteristic strength at 20°C equal to fyk = 260 MPa and the 
cross section of the beam is a SHS profile (squared hollow section) with dimension B = H = 300 mm and 
thickness t = 10mm. For the sake of simplicity, the consideration of the rounded corners of the profile can 
be neglected in the geometric calculations, i.e. the shape of the cross-section can be considered to be a 
perfect square.
-
The beam is exposed to fire on all sides and is placed in a compartment type D (50% concrete, 50% light-
concrete) having a thermal inertial b = 800 W∙s½∙m-2∙K-1. The ventilation of the compartment is O = 0.04 
m½ and the fuel load density is q = 200 MJ/m2 (surface of the enclosure). 
It is requested to calculate the minimum thickness of the insulating panel that is needed for the beam to sustain 
the imposed load during the whole duration of the fire. 
Please note that the automatic solution provided by SteFi will not be accepted and that, in all cases, the 
solution procedure together with formulas, calculations, and results must be reported in a tidy and readable 
form on a pdf file.

==PAGE:375==
Content
This document is a code report for the new developed optimization module and addition-
ally add-ons to the software SteFi. The report contains four scripts. The script SteFi.py
shows added code to the original script. optimization.py Contains all calculations for the
new optimization module and works as a back-end script. Report.py highlights added
code to the report module. Setup.py is a script used to create a exe for SteFi.
1
==PAGE:376==
1
Stefi.py
1
2

Optimization ##

GUI for SteFi ##

# Returns the critical temperature using Eurocode 3
12
def deterioration_temp_EC3(kyop):
13
14
if
kyop == 1:
15
Tsop = 400
16
17
elif
1 > kyop > 0.78:
18
Tsop = 400+(500-400)*(kyop -1.0)/(0.78-1.0)
19
20
elif
0.78 > kyop > 0.47:
21
Tsop = 500+(600-500)*(kyop -0.78)/(0.47-0.78)
22
23
elif
0.47 > kyop > 0.23:
24
Tsop = 600+(700-600)*(kyop -0.47)/(0.23-0.47)
25
26
elif
0.23 > kyop > 0.11:
27
Tsop = 700+(800-700)*(kyop -0.23)/(0.11-0.23)
28
29
elif
0.11 > kyop > 0.06:
30
Tsop = 800+(900-800)*(kyop -0.11)/(0.06-0.11)
31
32
elif
0.06 > kyop > 0.04:
33
Tsop = 900+(1000-900)*(kyop -0.06)/(0.04-0.06)
34
35
elif
0.04 > kyop > 0.02:
36
Tsop = 1000+(1100-1000)*(kyop -0.04)/(0.02-0.04)
37
38
elif
0.02 > kyop:
39
Tsop = 1100
40
41
return Tsop

# Returns the critical temperature using Hertz
44
def deterioration_temp_hertz(xiop,stressType):
45
46
Ts = np.arange(0,1100,0.01)
47
48
if stressType == "0.2":
49
k = 0
50
T1 = 6000
51
T2 = 620
27
==PAGE:402==
52
T8 = 565
53
T64 = 1100
54
55
for j in range(len(Ts)):
56
57
xi = k + (1-k)/(1+(Ts[j]/T1)+(Ts[j]/T2)**2+(Ts[j]/T8)**8+(Ts[j
]/T64)**64)
58
59
if xiop - xi > 0.002:
60
return Ts[j]
61
break
62
63
elif stressType == "2.0":
64
k = 0
65
T1 = 100000
66
T2 = 100000
67
T8 = 593
68
T64 = 100000
69
70
for j in range(len(Ts)):
71
72
xi = k + (1-k)/(1+(Ts[j]/T1)+(Ts[j]/T2)**2+(Ts[j]/T8)**8+(Ts[j
]/T64)**64)
73
74
if j == 109999:
75
Ts[j] = 1100
76

break

def Perimeter(selectedProfile , P2exposure_type , P3insulation_type , B, H, tw
, r, r2, D):

def BC1(data,fy,YM0,ME,P2element_type ,eta,Load,ls,Es,alpha):#

# Function to calculate price of profile
447
def price(Pst, Pin, rho, diRequired , Per,Area,selectedSprayedInsulationType
):
448
Pst = float(Pst);
449
Pin = float(Pin);
450
rho = float(rho);
451
#
if selectedSprayedInsulationType == 'Intumescent paint':
452
#
din = float(diRequired)#*0.008;
453
#
else:
454
din = float(diRequired)*10**(-3);
455
Area = float(Area)*10**(-6);
456
457
Psteel = Area * rho * Pst
458
Pinsulation = Per * din * Pin
459
460
P = Psteel + Pinsulation
461
462
return P, Psteel , Pinsulation
